<td>2016 (4th session)</td>

<p><b>Nowruz</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl" xml:lang="fa"></span> <i>Nowruz</i>; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Persian" title="Help:IPA for Persian">[nouuz]</a></span>; literally "New Day") is the name of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> <a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> also known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> New Year</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> which is celebrated worldwide by the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a>, along with some other ethno-linguistic groups, as the beginning of the New Year.</p>
<p>It has been celebrated for over 3,000 years in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sea Basin">Black Sea Basin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> It marks the first day of the first month (<a href="/wiki/Farvardin" title="Farvardin">Farvardin</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_calendars" title="Iranian calendars">Iranian calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nowruz is the day of the <a href="/wiki/Vernal_equinox" class="mw-redirect" title="Vernal equinox">vernal equinox</a>, and marks the beginning of spring in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. It usually occurs on <a href="/wiki/March_21" title="March 21">21 March</a> or the previous or following day, depending on where it is observed. The moment the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a> crosses the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a> and equalizes night and day is calculated exactly every year, and families gather together to observe the rituals.</p>
<p>Although having <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> and religious <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> origins, Nowruz has been celebrated by people from diverse ethno-linguistic communities for thousands of years. It is a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> holiday for most celebrants that is enjoyed by people of several different faiths, but remains a holy day for Zoroastrians.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term <i>Nowruz</i> is a Persian <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound</a> word, consisting of the words <i>now</i> and <i>ruz</i>. <i>Now</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Persian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Persian language">Old Persian</a>: <span lang="peo" xml:lang="peo"><i>nava</i></span>), which means "new" and descends from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/n%C3%A9wos" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/néwos"><i>*néos</i></a>, is cognate with <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> <i>new</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>neu</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>novus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>novyj</i> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>náva</i>. <i>Ruz</i> (<a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Persian language">Middle Persian</a>: <span lang="pal" xml:lang="pal"><i>rz</i>, <i>rj</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Avestan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Avestan language">Avestan</a>: <span lang="ae" xml:lang="ae"><i>raocah</i></span>), which means "day" in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Modern Persian</a>, is descended from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Iranian language">Proto-Iranian</a> <i>*rauah-</i>, itself derived from Proto-Indo-European <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/lewk-" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/lewk-"><i>*lek-</i></a>. The original meaning of the word, however, was "light". It is related to <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> <i>loys</i>, English <i>light</i>, Latin <i>lux</i>, Sanskrit <i>rúci</i> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Slovenian language">Slovenian</a> <i>lu</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variations_in_pronunciation">Regional variations in pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Regional variations in pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Persian pronunciation differs in the many dialects of the language. While the eastern dialects have preserved the <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a> <i>nau</i> (<small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Persian" title="Help:IPA for Persian">[næuoz]</a></span>), the western dialects usually pronounce it with the diphthong <i>now</i> (<small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Persian" title="Help:IPA for Persian">[nouuz]</a></span>), and some colloquial variants (such as the <a href="/wiki/Tehrani_accent" title="Tehrani accent">Tehrani accent</a>) pronounce it with a long <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a> (<small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Persian" title="Help:IPA for Persian">[nouz]</a></span>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_variations">Spelling variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spelling variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A variety of spelling variations for the word <i>Nowruz</i> exist in English-language usage. <a href="/wiki/Random_House" title="Random House">Random House</a> (unabridged) provides the spelling <i>Nowruz</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster" title="Merriam-Webster">Merriam-Webster</a> (2006) recognizes only the spelling "Nauruz" (and a contestant in the final session of the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Scripps_National_Spelling_Bee" title="Scripps National Spelling Bee">Scripps National Spelling Bee</a> in the United States, Allion Salvador, was disqualified on that basis).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_origin">History and origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History and origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_roots">Ancient roots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ancient roots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although it is not clear whether Proto-Indo-Iranians celebrated a feast as the first day of the calendar, there are indications that Iranians may have observed the beginning of both autumn and spring, respectively related to the harvest and the sowing of seeds, for the celebration of the New Year.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyce_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Boyce" title="Mary Boyce">Mary Boyce</a> and Frantz Grenet explain the traditions for seasonal festivals and comment: "It is possible that the splendor of the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Babylonian</a> festivities at this season led the Iranians to develop their own spring festival into an established New Year feast, with the name <i>Navasarda</i> "New Year" (a name which, though first attested through Middle Persian derivatives, is attributed to the Achaemenian period)." Since the communal observations of the ancient Iranians appear in general to have been a seasonal ones, and related to agriculture, "it is probable that they traditionally held festivals in both autumn and spring, to mark the major turning points of the natural year."<sup id="cite_ref-Boyce_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyce-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nowruz is partly rooted in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_religions" title="Iranian religions">Iranian religions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Mitraism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitraism">Mitraism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>. In Mitraism, festivals had a deep linkage with the sun's light. The Iranian festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Mehrgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehrgan">Mehrgan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Autumnal_equinox" class="mw-redirect" title="Autumnal equinox">autumnal equinox</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tirgan" title="Tirgan">Tirgan</a>, and the eve of <a href="/wiki/Yalda" class="mw-redirect" title="Yalda">Chelle ye Zemestan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a>) also had an origin in the Sun god (<a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a>). Among other ideas, Zoroastrianism is the first <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> religion that emphasizes broad concepts such as the corresponding work of good and evil in the world, and the connection of humans to nature. Zoroastrian practices were dominant for much of the history of ancient Iran. In Zoroastrianism, the seven most important Zoroastrian festivals are the six <a href="/wiki/Gahambars" title="Gahambars">Gahambar</a> festivals and Nowruz, which occurs at the <a href="/wiki/Spring_equinox" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring equinox">spring equinox</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Mary_Boyce" title="Mary Boyce">Mary Boyce</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> "It seems a reasonable surmise that Nowruz, the holiest of them all, with deep doctrinal significance, was founded by <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> himself"; although there is no clear date of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Between sunset on the day of the sixth Gahambar and sunrise of Nowruz, <a href="/wiki/Hamaspathmaedaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamaspathmaedaya">Hamaspathmaedaya</a> (later known, in its extended form, as <i>Frawardinegan</i>; and today known as <i>Farvardigan</i>) was celebrated. This and the Gahambars are the only festivals named in the surviving text of the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>.</p>
<p>The 10th-century scholar <a href="/wiki/Biruni" class="mw-redirect" title="Biruni">Biruni</a>, in his work <i>Kitab al-Tafhim li Awa'il Sina'at al-Tanjim</i>, provides a description of the calendars of various nations. Besides the Iranian calendar, various festivals of Greeks, Jews, Arabs, Sabians, and other nations are mentioned in the book. In the section on the Iranian calendar, he mentions Nowruz, <a href="/wiki/Sadeh" title="Sadeh">Sadeh</a>, Tirgan, Mehrgan, the six Gahambars, Farvardigan, Bahmanja, <a href="/wiki/Sepand%C4%81rmazg%C4%81n" title="Sepandrmazgn">Esfand Armaz</a> and several other festivals. According to him, "It is the belief of the Iranians that Nowruz marks the first day when the universe started its motion."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The Persian historian <a href="/wiki/Gardizi" class="mw-redirect" title="Gardizi">Gardizi</a>, in his work titled <i>Zayn al-Akhbr</i>, under the section of the Zoroastrians festivals, mentions Nowruz (among other festivals) and specifically points out that <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> highly emphasized the celebration of Nowruz and Mehrgan.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_period">Achaemenid period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Achaemenid period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg/220px-Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg/330px-Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg/440px-Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3509" data-file-height="2349" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Persepolis_stairs_of_the_Apadana_relief.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A bas-relief at the <a href="/wiki/Apadana" title="Apadana">Apadana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, depicting <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> bringing their famous wine to the king.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It has been suggested that the famous <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> complex, or at least the palace of <a href="/wiki/Apadana" title="Apadana">Apadana</a> and the Hundred Columns Hall, were built for the specific purpose of celebrating a feast related to Nowruz. Although there may be no mention of the term <i>Nowruz</i> in recorded Achaemenid inscriptions,<sup id="cite_ref-Khodadad_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khodadad-58">[58]</a></sup> there is a detailed account by <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> of a Nowruz celebration taking place in Persepolis and the continuity of this festival in the Achaemenid tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It was an important day during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenids</a> (<span class="nowrap">c. 550330 BCE</span>), where kings from different nations under the Achaemenid Empire used to bring gifts to the <a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">King of Kings</a> of Iran. The significance of the ceremony in the Achaemenid Empire was such that King <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a>'s appointment as the king of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> was legitimized only after his participation in the referred annual Achaemenid festival.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> It was, therefore, a highly auspicious occasion for the ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian peoples</a>.</p>
<p>In 539 BC, the Jews came under Iranian rule, thus exposing both groups to each other's customs. According to the <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a>, the story of <a href="/wiki/Purim" title="Purim">Purim</a> as told in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a> is adapted from an Iranian novella about the shrewdness of harem queens, suggesting that Purim may be a transformation of the Iranian New Year.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Britannica_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica-61">[61]</a></sup> A specific novella is not identified and Encyclopædia Britannica itself notes that "no Jewish texts of this genre from the Persian period are extant, so these new elements can be recognized only inferentially". The <a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Religion_and_Ethics" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopaedia of Religion and Ethics">Encyclopaedia of Religion and Ethics</a> notes that the Purim holiday is based on a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a>, while Nowruz occurs at the spring equinox (<a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar calendar</a>). The two holidays are therefore celebrated on different dates but within a few weeks of each other, depending on the year. Given their temporal associations, it is possible that the Jews and Iranians of the time may have shared or adopted similar customs for these holidays.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_of_Religion_and_Ethics_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_of_Religion_and_Ethics-62">[62]</a></sup> The story of Purim as told in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a> has been dated around 625465 BC (although the story takes place with the Jews under the rule of the Achaemenid Empire and the Jews had come under Iranian rule in 539 BC), while Nowruz is thought to have first been celebrated between 555330 BC. It remains unclear which holiday was established first.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arsacid_and_Sassanid_periods">Arsacid and Sassanid periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arsacid and Sassanid periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nowruz was the holiday of <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Arsacid</a> dynastic empires who ruled Iran (248224 CE) and the other areas ruled by the Arsacid dynasties outside of <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> (such as the Arsacid dynasties of <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_dynasty_of_Armenia" title="Arsacid dynasty of Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_dynasty_of_Iberia" title="Arsacid dynasty of Iberia">Iberia</a>). There are specific references to the celebration of Nowruz during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Vologases_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Vologases I">Vologases I</a> (5178 CE), but these include no details.<sup id="cite_ref-Khodadad_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khodadad-58">[58]</a></sup> Before <a href="/wiki/Sassanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanids">Sassanids</a> established their power in Western Asia around 300 CE, Parthians celebrated Nowruz in autumn, and the first of <a href="/wiki/Farvardin" title="Farvardin">Farvardin</a> began at the autumn equinox. During the reign of the Parthian dynasty, the spring festival was <a href="/wiki/Mehrgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehrgan">Mehrgan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> and Iranian festival celebrated in honor of <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Extensive records on the celebration of Nowruz appear following the accession of <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> (224651 CE). Under the Sassanid emperors, Nowruz was celebrated as the most important day of the year. Most royal traditions of Nowruz, such as royal audiences with the public, cash gifts, and the pardoning of prisoners, were established during the Sassanid era and persisted unchanged until modern times.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_Muslim_conquest">After the Muslim conquest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: After the Muslim conquest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nowruz, along with <a href="/wiki/Sadeh" title="Sadeh">Sadeh</a> (celebrated in mid-winter), survived in society after the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Iran</a> in 650 CE. Other celebrations such the <a href="/wiki/Gahambars" title="Gahambars">Gahambars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mehrgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehrgan">Mehrgan</a> were eventually side-lined or were only followed by the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrians">Zoroastrians</a> who carried them. It was adopted as the main royal holiday during the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> period.</p>
<p>In the book <i>Nowruznama</i> ("Book of the New Year", which is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Omar_Khayyam" title="Omar Khayyam">Omar Khayyam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> a well known <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian poet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>), a vivid description of the celebration in the courts of the kings of Iran is provided:<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> "From the era of <a href="/wiki/Kai_Khosrow" class="mw-redirect" title="Kai Khosrow">Kai Khosrow</a> till the days of <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_III" title="Yazdegerd III">Yazdegard</a>, last of the pre-Islamic kings of Iran, the royal custom was thus: on the first day of the New Year, <i>Now Ruz</i>, the king's first visitor was the High <a href="/wiki/Mobad" title="Mobad">Mobad</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrians">Zoroastrians</a>, who brought with him as gifts a golden goblet full of wine, a ring, some gold coins, a fistful of green sprigs of wheat, a sword, and a bow. In the language of Iran, he would then glorify God and praise the monarch. This was the address of the High Mobad to the king: "O <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Majesty</a>, on this feast of the <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinox</a>, first day of the first month of the year, seeing that thou hast freely chosen God and the faith of the ancient ones; may <a href="/wiki/Sraosha" title="Sraosha">Sraosha</a>, the angel-messenger, grant thee wisdom and insight and sagacity in thy affairs. Live long in praise, be happy and fortunate upon thy golden throne, drink immortality from the <i>Cup of <a href="/wiki/Jamshid" title="Jamshid">Jamshid</a></i>; and keep in solemn trust the customs of our ancestors, their noble aspirations, fair gestures and the exercise of justice and righteousness. May thy soul flourish; may thy youth be as the new-grown grain; may thy horse be puissant, victorious; thy sword bright and deadly against foes; thy hawk swift against its prey; thy every act straight as the arrow's shaft. Go forth from thy rich throne, conquer new lands. Honor the craftsman and the sage in equal degree; disdain the acquisition of wealth. May thy house prosper and thy life be long!"</p>
<p>Following the demise of the caliphate and the subsequent re-emergence of Iranian dynasties such as the <a href="/wiki/Samanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Samanids">Samanids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buyids" class="mw-redirect" title="Buyids">Buyids</a>, Nowruz was elevated to an even more important event. The Buyids revived the ancient traditions of Sassanian times and restored many smaller celebrations that had been eliminated by the caliphate. According to the Syrian historian <a href="/wiki/Yaqut_al-Hamawi" title="Yaqut al-Hamawi">Yaqut al-Hamawi</a>, the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Buyid" class="mw-redirect" title="Buyid">Buyid</a> ruler Aod-od-Dawla (r. 949-83) customarily welcomed Nowruz in a majestic hall, wherein servants had placed gold and silver plates and vases full of fruit and colorful flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-IranicaShahbazi_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IranicaShahbazi-66">[66]</a></sup> The King would sit on the royal throne (<i>masnad</i>), and the court astronomer came forward, kissed the ground, and congratulated him on the arrival of the New Year.<sup id="cite_ref-IranicaShahbazi_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IranicaShahbazi-66">[66]</a></sup> The king would then summon musicians and singers, and invited his boon companions. They would gather in their assigned places and enjoy a great festive occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-IranicaShahbazi_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IranicaShahbazi-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> invaders did not attempt to abolish Nowruz in favor of any other celebration. Thus, Nowruz remained as the main celebration in Iranian lands by both the officials and the people.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 229.33333333333px">
<div style="width: 229.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 227.33333333333px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shah_Abbas_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/341px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg" width="228" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/511px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/682px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="541" /></a></div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_era">Contemporary era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Contemporary era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, Iran was the only country that officially observed the ceremonies of Nowruz. When the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> countries gained independence from the Soviets, they also declared Nowruz as a national holiday.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">UN's General Assembly</a> recognized the International Day of Nowruz in 2010, describing it as a spring festival of Iranian origin, which has been celebrated for over 3,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> During the meeting of <i>The Inter-governmental Committee for the Safeguarding of the Intangible Heritage</i> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, held between 28 September&#160; 2 October 2009, Nowruz was officially registered on the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_Intangible_Cultural_Heritage_Lists" title="UNESCO Intangible Cultural Heritage Lists">UNESCO List of the Intangible Cultural Heritage of Humanity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In response to the UN recognition, Iran unveiled a postage stamp. The stamp was made public in the presence of the Iranian President during the first International Nowruz Celebrations in Tehran on Saturday, 27 March 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second International Nowruz Celebrations were also held in Tehran in 2011. The 3rd International Nowruz Celebrations were held in Dushanbe, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, on March 25, 2012 with Tajik President and his Iranian and Afghan counterparts in attendance. The next international ceremonies to celebrate Nowruz were scheduled to be hosted by <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Locality">Locality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Locality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The festival of Nowruz is celebrated by many groups of people in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> basin, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Asia">southern</a> Asia, and by <a href="/wiki/Iranian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian people">Iranians</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Countries that have Nowruz as a public holiday include:</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan"><img alt="Afghanistan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/45px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> (21 March)<sup id="cite_ref-Goodsell_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodsell-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania"><img alt="Albania" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Flag_of_Albania.svg/21px-Flag_of_Albania.svg.png" width="21" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Flag_of_Albania.svg/32px-Flag_of_Albania.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Flag_of_Albania.svg/42px-Flag_of_Albania.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="500" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> (22 March)<sup id="cite_ref-komunitetibektashi.org_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-komunitetibektashi.org-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan"><img alt="Azerbaijan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg/46px-Flag_of_Azerbaijan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> (20 March to 26 March, total of seven days)<sup id="cite_ref-azerbaijan_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-azerbaijan-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Azerbaijan_2010_Bank_Holidays_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Azerbaijan_2010_Bank_Holidays-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)"><img alt="Georgia (country)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Georgia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Georgia.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Georgia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Georgia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Georgia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Georgia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a><sup id="cite_ref-civilcivil_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-civilcivil-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran"><img alt="Iran" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flag_of_Iran.svg/23px-Flag_of_Iran.svg.png" width="23" height="13" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flag_of_Iran.svg/35px-Flag_of_Iran.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flag_of_Iran.svg/46px-Flag_of_Iran.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="360" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (20 March to 24 March, total of five days in general and total of 14 days for schools and universities)<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq"><img alt="Iraq" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Flag_of_Iraq.svg/23px-Flag_of_Iraq.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Flag_of_Iraq.svg/35px-Flag_of_Iraq.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Flag_of_Iraq.svg/45px-Flag_of_Iraq.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="411" data-file-height="274" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> (<i>de jure</i> in <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan"><img alt="Iraqi Kurdistan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg/45px-Flag_of_Kurdistan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Iraqi Kurdistan</a>, <i>de facto</i> national)<sup id="cite_ref-IMFA_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMFA-6">[6]</a></sup> (21 March)<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan"><img alt="Kazakhstan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg/46px-Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="300" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> (21 March to 24 March, total of four days)<sup id="cite_ref-stan_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stan-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo"><img alt="Kosovo" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Flag_of_Kosovo.svg/21px-Flag_of_Kosovo.svg.png" width="21" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Flag_of_Kosovo.svg/32px-Flag_of_Kosovo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Flag_of_Kosovo.svg/42px-Flag_of_Kosovo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> (21 March)</li>

<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia"><img alt="Mongolia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Mongolia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Mongolia.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Mongolia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Mongolia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Mongolia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Mongolia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="4800" data-file-height="2400" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Bayan-%C3%96lgii" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayan-Ölgii">Bayan-Ölgii</a>, Mongolia (22 March, regional state holiday only)<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://discover-bayanolgii.com/">[3]</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan"><img alt="Tajikistan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg/46px-Flag_of_Tajikistan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> (20 March to 23 March, total of four days)<sup id="cite_ref-tajikistan_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tajikistan-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>

</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadian</a> parliament, on 30 March 2009, by unanimous consent, passed a bill to add Nowruz to the national calendar of Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-PRESSTV_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PRESSTV-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nowruz is celebrated by <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish people</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a><sup id="cite_ref-IMFA_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMFA-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> as well as by the <a href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.</p>
<p>It is also taken place by <a href="/wiki/Iranian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian people">Iranian</a> communities in several regions in Europe and the Americas, including <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> But because Los Angeles is prone to devastating fires, there are very strict fire codes in the city. No fires are allowed even on one's own property. Usually, Iranians living in Southern California go to the beaches to celebrate the event where it is permissible to build fires.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> On 15 March 2010, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Representatives of the United States">House of Representatives of the United States</a> passed the <i>Nowruz Resolution</i> (H.Res. 267), by a 3842 vote,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> "Recognizing the cultural and historical significance of Nowruz, ... ."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182.66666666667px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nowruz is the most important holiday in Iran, marking the official New Year of the country. It is the first day of <a href="/wiki/Farvardin" title="Farvardin">Farvardin</a>, the first month of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Iranian solar calendar</a>. In Iran, families celebrate the New Year since the exact time of the <a href="/wiki/March_equinox" title="March equinox">March equinox</a>, which is calculated every year.</p>
<p>In Iran, following the <a href="/wiki/1979_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="1979 Revolution">1979 Revolution</a>, some elements from the government have attempted to suppress Nowruz with very little success. These considered Nowruz a pagan holiday and a distraction from <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holidays" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic holidays">Islamic holidays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Azerbaijan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Novruz_in_Azerbaijan" title="Novruz in Azerbaijan">Novruz in Azerbaijan</a></div>
<p>After Iran, the Republic of Azerbaijan hosts the greatest number of public holidays related to Nowruz, with a total of seven days.<sup id="cite_ref-Azerbaijan_2010_Bank_Holidays_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Azerbaijan_2010_Bank_Holidays-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Azerbaijan, the holiday goes on for several days and ends with festive public dancing and other entertainment of folk bands, as well as the contests of national sports. In rural areas, crop holidays are also marked.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>

<p>The Azeri diaspora has communal associations that organize Nowruz celebrations in the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://azeriamericanews.com/tag/nowruz-holiday/">US and Canada</a> and in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://vestnikkavkaza.net/articles/society/68622.html">Israel</a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Afghanistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nowruz_in_Afghanistan" title="Nowruz in Afghanistan">Nowruz in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Nauruz is celebrated widely in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. Also known as the <i>Farmer's Day</i>, the observances usually last two weeks, culminating on the first day of the Afghan New Year, on March 21.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodsell_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodsell-24">[24]</a></sup> During the Taliban rule (19962001), Nauruz was banned and considered "an ancient pagan holiday centered on fire worship".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Albania">Albania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Albania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, the day of <i>Sultan Nevruzit</i> is celebrated as a mainly mystical day by the <a href="/wiki/Bektashi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bektashi">Bektashi</a> sect. There are special ceremonies in the <a href="/wiki/Zawiyya" class="mw-redirect" title="Zawiyya">Tekke</a>, led by the clergy, and large meals are served there. They celebrate this day as the birthday of <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>.</p>
<p>Alongside the religious celebration, all Albanians celebrate a secular version of Nowruz, which is referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Spring_Day" title="Spring Day">Spring Day</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>: <span lang="sq" xml:lang="sq"><i>Dita e Verës</i></span>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenia">Armenia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Armenia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the extinction during the 19th century, Nowruz is not celebrated by <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> and is not a public holiday in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>. However, it is celebrated in Armenia by tens of thousands of Iranian tourists who visit Armenia with relative ease.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The influx of tourists from Iran accelerated since around 201011.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In 2010 alone, around 27,600 Iranians spent Nowruz in capital <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">Yerevan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, President <a href="/wiki/Serzh_Sargsyan" title="Serzh Sargsyan">Serzh Sargsyan</a> sent a letter of congratulations to <a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Armenia" title="Kurds in Armenia">Kurds living in Armenia</a> and to the Iranian political leadership on the occasion of Nowruz.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, Nowruz is celebrated mainly in China's <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Uyghur_Autonomous_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region">Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajiks_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Tajiks in China">Chinese Tajik</a>, Salar, and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakh</a> ethnicities.<sup id="cite_ref-xinhuanet.com_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xinhuanet.com-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>It's a tradition for people to plant trees, dredge irrigation canals, clean houses and prepare scrumptious food for guests during the festival.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia">Georgia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Georgia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nowruz is not celebrated by <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgians</a> (excluding those who live in <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Georgians" title="Iranian Georgians">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ingiloy_people" title="Ingiloy people">Azerbaijan</a>), but it has become a public holiday in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> since 2010. It is widely celebrated by the country's large <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijanis_in_Georgia" title="Azerbaijanis in Georgia">Azerbaijani minority</a> (~7% of the total population)<sup id="cite_ref-agenda.ge_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agenda.ge-103">[103]</a></sup> as well as by the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranians</a> living in Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-agenda.ge_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agenda.ge-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Most Georgian Azerbaijanis live in <a href="/wiki/Kvemo_Kartli" title="Kvemo Kartli">Kvemo Kartli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kakheti" title="Kakheti">Kakheti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shida_Kartli" title="Shida Kartli">Shida Kartli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mtskheta-Mtianeti" title="Mtskheta-Mtianeti">Mtskheta-Mtianeti</a> regions. In addition, there is also a large historical Azerbaijani community in the capital city of Tbilisi, thus marking these as the core regions of celebration in Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-agenda.ge_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agenda.ge-103">[103]</a></sup> Every year, large festivities are held notably in the capital <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-agenda.ge_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agenda.ge-103">[103]</a></sup> Georgian politicians have attended the festivities in the capital over the years, and have congratulated the Nowruz-observing ethnic groups and nationals in Georgia on the day of Nowruz.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kurds">Kurds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kurds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Newroz_as_celebrated_by_Kurds" title="Newroz as celebrated by Kurds">Newroz as celebrated by Kurds</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newroz_Istanbul(5).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg/220px-Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg/330px-Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg/440px-Newroz_Istanbul%285%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2484" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newroz_Istanbul(5).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Newroz in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Newroz (or <i>Nevruz</i>) is largely considered as a potent symbol of Kurdish identity in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, even if there are some <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> (including Turkmens) celebrating the festival. The Kurds of Turkey celebrate this feast between 18th till 21 March. Kurds gather into fairgrounds mostly outside the cities to welcome spring. Women wear colored dresses and spangled head scarves and young men wave flags of green, yellow and red, the historic colors of Kurdish people. They hold this festival by lighting fire and dancing around it.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Newroz celebrations are usually organised by Kurdish cultural associations and pro-Kurdish political parties. Thus, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Society_Party" title="Democratic Society Party">Democratic Society Party</a> was a leading force in the organisation of the 2006 Newroz events throughout Turkey. In recent years, the Newroz celebration gathers around 1 million participants in <a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakr">Diyarbakr</a>, the biggest city of the Kurdish dominated Southeastern Turkey. As the Kurdish Newroz celebrations in Turkey often are theater for political messages, the events are frequently criticized for being political rallies rather than cultural celebrations.</p>
<p>Until 2005, the Kurdish population of Turkey could not celebrate their New Year openly.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> "Thousands of people have been detained in Turkey, as the authorities take action against suspected supporters of the Kurdish rebel movement, the PKK.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The holiday is now official in Turkey after international pressure on the Turkish government to lift culture bans. Turkish government renamed the holiday <i>Nevroz</i> in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In the recent years, limitations on expressions of Kurdish national identity, including the usage of <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a> in the public sphere, have been considerably relaxed.</p>
<p>On 21 March 2013, PKK leader <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Ocalan" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdullah Ocalan">Abdullah Ocalan</a> called for a ceasefire through a message that was released in <a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakr">Diyarbakr</a> during the Newroz celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ocalan_calls_for_ceasefire_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ocalan_calls_for_ceasefire-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Syria, the Kurds dress up in their national dress and celebrate the New Year.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> According to Human Rights Watch, the Kurds have had to struggle to celebrate Newroz, and in the past the celebration has led to violent oppression, leading to several deaths and mass arrests.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The government has stated that the Newroz celebrations will be tolerated as long as they do not become political demonstrations of the treatment of the Kurds.<sup id="cite_ref-Yildiz_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yildiz-114">[114]</a></sup> During the Newroz celebrations in 2008, three Kurds were shot dead by Syrian security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kurds in the <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> also celebrate the New Year; for example, Kurds in Australia celebrate Newroz, not only as the beginning of the new year, but also as the Kurdish National Day. The Kurds in Finland celebrate the new year as a way of demonstrating their support for the Kurdish cause.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Also in London, organizers estimated that 25000 people celebrated Newroz during March 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nowruz celebrations are observed by some of the ethnic groups in Pakistan, such as the Pashtuns and Baloch people. It may last weeks,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> particularly in the states of Khyber Pakhtunkhwa and Balochistan, from where the largest groups of Pakistan's Iranian-speaking people originate from (Pashtuns and Balochis respectively).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parsis_.28Indian_subcontinent.29">Parsis (Indian subcontinent)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Parsis (Indian subcontinent)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Parsi New Year is celebrated as <i>Jamshed Navroz</i> by the entire <a href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsi</a> community across the world. The festival falls on the first day of the first month of the <a href="/wiki/Fasli_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Fasli calendar">Fasli calendar</a>, followed by the Parsis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This falls in the month of March, according to the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. As the day commences with the advent of spring or the vernal equinox, Jamshed Navroz is celebrated with immense fun and fervor. Zoroastrian Parsis observe the festival by performing the rituals and rites with full devotion and duty. A particular sect of Parsis resides in the western part of India and hence, Jamshed Navroz celebrations can be prominently noticed in these regions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observances">Observances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Observances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_cleaning_and_shopping">House cleaning and shopping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: House cleaning and shopping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spring_cleaning" title="Spring cleaning">Spring cleaning</a></div>
<p>House cleaning, or <i>shaking the house</i> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"> </span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>xane tekni</i></span>) as referred to by Persian-speaking Iranians, is commonly observed before the arrival of Nowruz. People start preparing for Nowruz with a major spring-cleaning of their house and the purchase of new clothes to wear for the New Year, as well the purchase of flowers. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Hyacinth_(plant)" title="Hyacinth (plant)">hyacinth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tulip" title="Tulip">tulip</a> are popular and conspicuous.</p>
<p>In association with the "rebirth of nature", extensive spring cleaning is a national tradition observed by almost every household in Iran. This is also extended to personal attire, and it is customary to buy at least one set of new clothes.</p>
<p>Parsis adorn their houses with different auspicious symbols; namely, stars, butterflies, birds and fish. They order and make new attires especially for the festival. On the day of Navroz, they dress in their new and best clothes and put on gold and silver kustis and caps. They decorate the doors and windows with garlands of roses and jasmines, and use color powders for creating patterns known as <i>rangoli</i> on the steps and thresholds. Fish and floral motifs are a favorite among rangolis and considered highly auspicious.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festival_of_Charshanbe_Suri">Festival of Charshanbe Suri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Festival of Charshanbe Suri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chaharshanbe_Suri" title="Chaharshanbe Suri">Charshanbe Suri</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png/220px-Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png/330px-Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png/440px-Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png 2x" data-file-width="2490" data-file-height="2159" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chaharshanbe_Suri_Lozupone_NYC_2016_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charshanbe_Suri" class="mw-redirect" title="Charshanbe Suri">Charshanbe Suri</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, March 2016</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charshanbe_Suri" class="mw-redirect" title="Charshanbe Suri">Charshanbe Suri</a> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl" xml:lang="fa"> </span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>ranbe Suri</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>: <span lang="ku" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ku"><i>Çarema Sor</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>: <span lang="az" xml:lang="az"><i>Çrnb Bayram</i></span>) is a prelude to the New Year. In Iran, it is celebrated on the eve of the last Wednesday before Nowruz. It is usually celebrated in the evening, and is obtained by people making bonfires and jumping over them, as well as setting off <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> and sparklers.</p>
<p>In Azerbaijan, where the preparation for Novruz usually begins a month earlier, the festival is held every Tuesday during four weeks before the holiday of Novruz. Each Tuesday, people celebrate the day of one of the four elements&#160; water, fire, earth and wind.<sup id="cite_ref-nowaze_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nowaze-119">[119]</a></sup> On the holiday eve, the graves of relatives are visited and tended.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iranians sing the traditional poetic quote <i>zardi ye man az to, sorkhi ye to az man</i> during the festival, which literally means "my yellow is yours, your red is mine"; meaning you want the fire to replace your pallor, sickness, and problems with warmth and energy. <a href="/wiki/Trail_mix" title="Trail mix">Trail mix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berries" class="mw-redirect" title="Berries">berries</a> are also served during the celebration.</p>
<p>Spoon hitting (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"> </span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>qoq zani</i></span>) is an Iranian tradition observed on the eve of Charshanbe Suri, which is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Halloween" title="Halloween">Halloween</a> custom <a href="/wiki/Trick-or-treating" title="Trick-or-treating">Trick-or-treating</a>. It is practiced by people wearing <a href="/wiki/Disguise" title="Disguise">disguises</a> and going door-to-door to bang spoons against plates or bowls and receive packaged <a href="/wiki/Snack" title="Snack">snacks</a>.</p>
<p>In Azerbaijan, according to old traditions, children slip around to their neighbors' homes and apartments on the last Tuesday prior to Novruz, knock at the doors, and leave their caps or little basket on the thresholds all the while hiding nearby waiting for candies, pastries and nuts.<sup id="cite_ref-nowaze_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nowaze-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ritual of jumping over fire has remained in Armenia in the feast of <i><a href="/wiki/Trndez" title="Trndez">Trndez</a></i>, which is a feast of purification in the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic_Church" title="Armenian Catholic Church">Armenian Catholic Churches</a>, celebrated forty days after <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>'s birth.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidis</a>, this festival is celebrated on the first Wednesday of Nisan (April) which marks their New Year's Day.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals_of_Gul-i-Surkh_and_Dehq.C4.81n">Festivals of Gul-i-Surkh and Dehqn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Festivals of Gul-i-Surkh and Dehqn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Afghanistan, the festival of Gul-i-Surkh (<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span lang="prs" dir="rtl" xml:lang="prs"> </span>; "Red Flower," referring to red <a href="/wiki/Tulip" title="Tulip">tulip</a> flowers) is the principal festival for Nauruz. It is celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a> during the first 40 days of the year, when the tulip flowers grow in the green plains and over the hills surrounding the city. People from all over the country travel to Mazar-i-Sharif to attend the Nauruz festivals. Various activities and customs are performed during the Gul-i-Surkh festival, including:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Jahenda Bala</i> (<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span lang="prs" dir="rtl" xml:lang="prs"> </span>; "Raising"), which is celebrated on the first day of the New Year,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and is attended by high-ranking government officials such as the Vice-President, Ministers, and Provincial Governors  It is a specific religious ceremony performed at the <a href="/wiki/Shrine_of_Ali" title="Shrine of Ali">Blue Mosque</a> of Mazar-i-Sharif. The ceremony is performed by raising a special banner whose color configuration resembles <a href="/wiki/Derafsh_Kaviani" title="Derafsh Kaviani">Derafsh Kaviani</a>. This is the biggest recorded Nawroz gathering where up to 200,000 people from all over Afghanistan get together to celebrate the ceremony.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Buzkashi" title="Buzkashi">Buzkashi</a></i> tournament, held during the Gul-i-Surkh festival in Mazar-i-Sharif, <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> and other northern cities of Afghanistan</li>
</ul>
<p>The festival of <i>Dehqn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span lang="prs" dir="rtl" xml:lang="prs"></span>; "Farmer") is celebrated on the first day of the New Year, in which the farmers walk in the cities as a sign of encouragement for the agricultural productions. In recent years, this activity is being performed only in Kabul and other major cities of Afghanistan, in which the mayor and other high governmental personalities participate for watching and observing.</p>
<p>The citizens of <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> go to <a href="/wiki/Istalif" title="Istalif">Istalif</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charikar" title="Charikar">Charikar</a> or other green places around where the <a href="/wiki/Cercis" title="Cercis">Cercis</a> flowers grow. They go for picnic with their families during the first two weeks of the New Year.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_tables">Decorative tables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Decorative tables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Haft_Seen">Haft Seen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Haft Seen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haft_Seen" class="mw-redirect" title="Haft Seen">Haft Seen</a></div>
<p>Haft Seen (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl" xml:lang="fa"> </span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>Haft Sin</i></span>); "Seven S's") is the traditional <a href="/wiki/Table_setting" title="Table setting">table setting</a> of Nowruz in Iran. Typically, before the arrival of Nowruz, family members gather around a table, with the Haft Seen set on it, and await the exact moment of the <a href="/wiki/March_equinox" title="March equinox">March equinox</a> to celebrate the New Year. At that time, the New Year gifts are exchanged.</p>
<p>The setting includes seven items starting with the letter <i>S</i> or <i>seen</i> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>) in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">Perso-Arabic alphabet</a>. The items include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Greenery (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>sabze</i></span>): <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">Wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentil</a> sprouts grown in a dish</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samanu" title="Samanu">Samanu</a> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>samanu</i></span>): A sweet pudding made from germinated wheat</li>
<li>The dried fruit of the <a href="/wiki/Elaeagnus_angustifolia" title="Elaeagnus angustifolia">oleaster</a> tree (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>senjed</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Garlic" title="Garlic">Garlic</a> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>sir</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Apples" class="mw-redirect" title="Apples">Apples</a> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>sib</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sumac" title="Sumac">Sumac</a> berries (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>somq</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">Vinegar</a> (<span lang="fa" xml:lang="fa"></span>  <span title="Persian transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>serke</i></span>)</li>
</ul>
<p>These items are also known to have astrological correlations to planets Mercury, Venus, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Sun and Moon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Other symbolic items which are usually set along the Haft Seen are <a href="/wiki/Candle" title="Candle">candles</a>, a mirror, decorating coins, and <a href="/wiki/Egg_decorating" title="Egg decorating">decorated eggs</a> (sometimes one for each member of the family). A bowl of water with <a href="/wiki/Goldfish" title="Goldfish">goldfish</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> a holy book (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> or <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>) and/or a poetry book (e.g. the Divan of <a href="/wiki/Hafez" title="Hafez">Hafez</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Rose_water" title="Rose water">rose water</a> are also included to the setting.</p>
<p>The custom and the traditional practice of Haft Seen has been changed over times. The initial term <i>Haft Chin</i> meaning "the seven collected", has been gradually altered to the present-day name of the setting.</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Haft_Mewa">Haft Mewa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Haft Mewa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Afghanistan, people prepare Haft Mewa (<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span lang="prs" dir="rtl" xml:lang="prs"> </span>  <span title="Dari transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>Haft Mwa</i></span>; literally "the Seven Fruits") for the New Year's Day. It is similar to a <a href="/wiki/Fruit_salad" title="Fruit salad">fruit salad</a>, and is made from seven different <a href="/wiki/Dried_fruit" title="Dried fruit">dried fruits</a> served in their own <a href="/wiki/Syrup" title="Syrup">syrup</a>.</p>
<p>The seven dried fruits prepared for the Haft Mewa setting include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">Raisin</a></li>
<li>Senjed, the dried fruit of the <a href="/wiki/Elaeagnus_angustifolia" title="Elaeagnus angustifolia">oleaster</a> tree.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pistachio" title="Pistachio">Pistachio</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Common_Hazel" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Hazel">Hazelnut</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prune_(fruit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Prune (fruit)">Prune</a>, the dry fruit of <a href="/wiki/Apricot" title="Apricot">apricot</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">Walnut</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">Almond</a> or other species of <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plum</a> fruits.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Khoncha">Khoncha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nowruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Khoncha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Azerbaijan, the decoration of the festive table is called <i>Khoncha</i> (<a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>: <span lang="az" xml:lang="az"><i>Xonça</i></span>). It consists of a big silver or copper tray, with <a href="/wiki/Samani" title="Samani">Samani</a> placed in the center, as well as candles and dyed eggs by the number of family members around it.</p>
<p>The table should be set, at least, with seven dishes.<sup id="cite_ref-nowaze_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nowaze-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
